<div id="review_570746152" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/570746152" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/570746152?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=political">political</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=dystopia-yes-pls">dystopia-yes-pls</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=2017-shelf">2017-shelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=worldbuilding-sf">worldbuilding-sf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer570746152" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17093605540543572679">The first time I read this book back in the early nineties, I would have given it a four star rating because I was slightly annoyed with the prose and the steadily boring pace where nothing really big happens (mostly) except a general living of a life. This is despite our following a very interesting character escaping his pragmatic moon to gift his very advanced physics that would lead to not only an ansible for faster-than-light communications but also faster-than-light travel.<br/><br/>The world-buildi</span>
  <span id="freeText17093605540543572679" style="display:none">The first time I read this book back in the early nineties, I would have given it a four star rating because I was slightly annoyed with the prose and the steadily boring pace where nothing really big happens (mostly) except a general living of a life. This is despite our following a very interesting character escaping his pragmatic moon to gift his very advanced physics that would lead to not only an ansible for faster-than-light communications but also faster-than-light travel.<br /><br />The world-building is pretty amazing on both the political and socio-economic levels, the discussion of what men and women are to each other and just how amazingly different (and similar) it is between both worlds. The novel easily tackles six different heavy themes and does it with heart and no hammer in sight.<br /><br />On one hand, I know the author couldn't have tackled the whole gamut of two worlds without a very light touch, but it was this same light touch and frustrating lack of progress, the descent of the sense of utopia into desperate and dire dystopia, that eventually made me distrust this novel.<br /><br />It frankly took me two hundred pages, the first time, to even get into the novel. It requires a learning curve.<br /><br />Now that I'm reading this as a full adult with a lot of ideas under his belt, I eased into the read much more, expecting certain things and realizing it was primarily a novel of ideas and deep commentary. It's not just a political mirror or even a mirror between true communist idealism and anarchism. It's also a damn unique exploration of sexuality and how sexuality necessitates certain kinds of thinking, how a social structure informs it and how it can kill a real germination of ideas. <br /><br />I'm talking about two halves making a whole here. Men and women are just a half of it. The two political makeups of the moon and the planet aren't whole until they finally find a mix. It's Taoism and a mix of opposites and equals creating something more than the sum of its parts.<br /><br />And that's what is so tragic about this novel. There's distrust, revulsion against new thought, a nearly impossible wall between the sexes (and the obvious exception to that rule in this novel is noteworthy also because it occurs with the Dispossessed scientist). If people opened up their minds to new ideas, so much of this would have been avoided.<br /><br />During my original take, I was going to college at the time and I saw a lot of the same approbations and stifled thought in the academic arena. The Dispossessed brings up the plight of ourselves in science, the fact that certain ideas get heavily entrenched and new ones are mercilessly cut down at least until a new generation takes over.<br /><br />It all comes back to a germination of ideas. The call in the text to keep the flow of information going was really breathtaking, if not that unique. I think of the internet and how that has been such a boon to science now, but even in '92 when I read this, the weight of bureaucracy was immense. I'm sure things aren't all that different now. Aren't we still enamored with string theory and colliders and aren't we all getting rather upset that it hasn't been panning out as we would have liked? Well, alas, this isn't the forum for that but this book makes very good points all over the place.<br /><br />I ramble.<br /><br />The fact is, I'm increasing my rating on this book merely because it is gorgeous in conception and form. It carries on multiple narratives on so many aspects of our lives here and now and also within the fictional boundaries of political systems that don't exist anywhere except in our minds. She even goes on to conceive a world without cause and effect, where all things can and will be explored at the same time. How often can we have a cogent discussion about that, rooted firmly in the events of normal lives, and yet not have the text explode in handwavium and weird science? She keeps things real. And brilliant.<br /><br />I'm going to ignore my stylistic complaints and even the fact that I couldn't really get into it for hundreds of pages because the trip is more than impressive by the end. It's more of a monument to thought.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17093605540543572679" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_818792944" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/818792944" />
    <a title="Eric" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/76688-eric"><img alt="Eric" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1255373698p2/76688.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/818792944?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eric" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eric" href="/user/show/76688-eric">Eric</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Eric by: </span>
      Manny Citron
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76688-eric?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76688-eric?shelf=book-club">book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76688-eric?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76688-eric?shelf=classic">classic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer818792944" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13255522727235812978">This discourse on dystopias won Hugo, Nebula, Locus, World Fantasy, and National Book awards, and almost every single one of my Goodreads friends that has read it has it tagged with a 4 or 5 star rating. So clearly, the problem here is with me, because I really hated this book -- and it isn't because this book is dated or aged poorly, because the Cold War era slant of this book plays perfectly to a modern audience considering the current state of Russian-U.S. relations. <br/><br/>I'm giving it two stars b</span>
  <span id="freeText13255522727235812978" style="display:none">This discourse on dystopias won Hugo, Nebula, Locus, World Fantasy, and National Book awards, and almost every single one of my Goodreads friends that has read it has it tagged with a 4 or 5 star rating. So clearly, the problem here is with me, because I really hated this book -- and it isn't because this book is dated or aged poorly, because the Cold War era slant of this book plays perfectly to a modern audience considering the current state of Russian-U.S. relations. <br /><br />I'm giving it two stars because I do appreciate the big ideas Le Guin brings up. The vision behind the "profiteering" cultures of Urras -- with subdivisions for the capitalists of A-Io (U.S.) and the authoritarian state of the Thu (Russia) -- and the anarchist outcast settlement of Anarres was a solid and interesting foundation for the book. But the weak characterizations, uninspiring writing, unnecessarily non-linear storytelling, lack of action, and disappointing ending all added up to a very difficult and unrewarding reading experience for me. To address those points specifically (mild spoilers may follow):<br /><br />- There is only one character, Shevek, who is more than one-dimensional. The rest fill out the story as needed -- corrupt bureaucrat, radical friend, loving partner, etc. As for Shevek, for as brilliant as he is, he is naive to the point of incredulity. And I don't mean just after he leaves Anarres for A-Io. It takes him decades longer than his friends to see the corruption in his own anarchist world. He is willfully ignorant of what is going on around him for someone involved in something as deep as theoretical physics.<br /><br />- The writing was clunky throughout the entire novel, and had no rhythm. There were tedious lists, long sections of discourse about the various imperfections in the various imperfect societies, and unnecessary word invention -- although I will grant calling the toilet a shittery is funny, if nothing else.<br /><br />- Another aspect of the storytelling that did not agree with me was the alternating chapters, where one chapter would be a flashback to Anarres, and the next a current day chapter on Urras. I would have minded this less if anything interesting or noteworthy happened on Anarres -- what little did happen could have easily been worked into flashbacks in the current day chapters, which could have greatly shortened the novel, and likely, my enjoyment of it.<br /><br />- There was one action scene in entire novel, and, if you include the aftermath, maybe ten pages are spent on it in total. There were also two other scenes that contained somewhat tense conflict. I don't need every book I read to be paced like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2767052.The_Hunger_Games__The_Hunger_Games___1_" title="The Hunger Games (The Hunger Games, #1) by Suzanne Collins" rel="nofollow noopener">The Hunger Games</a>, but I need more of an action-driven plot than this, especially if you expect me to sit through endless info dumps on your imaginary dystopias.<br /><br />- The book ends right before another action scene -- or at least a scene with great potential for conflict -- that Le Guin either didn't know how to write her way out of, or didn't want to go out on a limb and make a stand for, which I see as a cop-out either way.<br /><br />- The overall feeling I was left with after reading this book was that capitalism sucks, anarchism sucks in different ways, and the only hope forward lies in benevolent aliens. This could have been improved if the ending to the novel went one chapter further, however it turned out.<br /><br />I could go on, but I believe my opinion is already more than clear. I will leave you with a quote from this book that sums up how I felt about reading it:<blockquote>He tried to read an elementary economics text; it bored him past endurance, it was like listening to somebody interminably recounting a long and stupid dream.</blockquote>Goodreads friends, in all seriousness, tell me what I am missing that led you to rate this so highly. I feel like I am the only one seeing the Emperor's bare ass here.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13255522727235812978" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781857988826" href="/book/show/756970.The_Dispossessed">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=1001-books-to-read-before-you-die">1001-books-to-read-before-you-die</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2447488873" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10008169303751096685">We humans really fail at the system level, don't we? <br/> <br/>Try as we might to make a fair collective of independent individuals, we invariably end up crushing those individuals in the process of distributing our homemade booty of choice according to increasingly corrupt rules and guidelines that keep changing in favour of those who happen to have the power to write them down.<br/><br/>The Dispossessed are everywhere. That they dispossess in their specific lives depends on the quirky path of history. It never s</span>
  <span id="freeText10008169303751096685" style="display:none">We humans really fail at the system level, don't we? <br /> <br />Try as we might to make a fair collective of independent individuals, we invariably end up crushing those individuals in the process of distributing our homemade booty of choice according to increasingly corrupt rules and guidelines that keep changing in favour of those who happen to have the power to write them down.<br /><br />The Dispossessed are everywhere. That they dispossess in their specific lives depends on the quirky path of history. It never stops amazing me that science fiction from remote decades in the past century still remains so accurate and heartfelt and true, despite the chronological movement forwards that has allowed (or should have allowed!) change to happen. Maybe Shevek's temporalist ideas are accurate for fiction at least? Maybe the past and the future and the strange in between that we call present - for lack of words to describe what is never really there - are simultaneously experienced whenever we read a book of this kind, reflecting on us and our present ordeal and on the future and its scary prospects and on the past and its terrifying lessons not learnt?<br /><br />And what unites us in reading is not a passionate alignment with the dogma of Urras or Anarres, but rather a sad nod in recognition that neither system worked as a one-for-all boiler plate to make humans live a decent and occasionally happy life. <br /><br />A lovely, well written and exciting story that contains more food for thought than meets the eye. Recommended to Urras and Anarres alike, for we are all the stuff that dreams are made on...</span>

<div id="review_2097521773" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2097521773" />
    <a title="Tara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70459127-tara"><img alt="Tara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592666421p2/70459127.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2097521773?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tara" href="/user/show/70459127-tara">Tara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Tara by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/38230974-junta">Junta</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70459127-tara?shelf=1001-list">1001-list</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2097521773" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13575707432309935772">I’ve gotta admit, I was initially a little wary of <i>The Dispossessed</i>. I was worried that it might prove to be one of those godawful books whose sole purpose in life is to peddle insipidly idealistic ideology. The derisive cynic in me was prepared for the worst, ready to swoop in with some aggressively bratty eye-rolling and loud, obnoxious sighing at the first sign of trouble. I mean, is there anything worse than those agenda-pushing heaps of garbage that masquerade as literature? Seriously, who </span>
  <span id="freeText13575707432309935772" style="display:none">I’ve gotta admit, I was initially a little wary of <i>The Dispossessed</i>. I was worried that it might prove to be one of those godawful books whose sole purpose in life is to peddle insipidly idealistic ideology. The derisive cynic in me was prepared for the worst, ready to swoop in with some aggressively bratty eye-rolling and loud, obnoxious sighing at the first sign of trouble. I mean, is there anything worse than those agenda-pushing heaps of garbage that masquerade as literature? Seriously, who wants to be force-fed blandly pious, naively optimistic kumbaya-type nonsense, even if it’s got a super sweet science fiction candy coating? Lowlife degenerates, that's who, and NOT the good kind.<br /><br />Fortunately, however, I was entirely wrong in this case. The political content comprised merely one dimension of a profoundly complex, multifaceted work; there is much more being offered here. Granted, Le Guin did have a lot to say about society and politics (shock horror!), but even so, that wasn’t what the book was primarily concerned with. Above all else, her focus, her passion, was <i>humanity</i>. The characters she created, and the fundamental concerns they wrestled with, were overwhelmingly, honestly, beautifully HUMAN. <br /><br />So yeah, this book had heart. It had Compassion and Integrity and Hope and Sincerity and Goodwill Toward Men and all that shit. But don’t fret, it somehow managed to pull this off in a non-Chicken Soup for the Idiots, nauseatingly mawkish way. This is remarkably difficult to do, and it certainly impressed the hell out of me. Then again, maybe I’m just getting soft.<br /><br />At any rate, I’m including a handful of her most insightful, thought-provoking specimens below:<br /><br /><b>On the innate separateness of individuals:</b><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“Freedom is that recognition of each person’s solitude which alone transcends it.”</i>
</blockquote><br /><b>On the dangers of conformity:</b><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“Kimoe’s ideas never seemed to be able to go in a straight line; they had to walk around this and avoid that, and then they ended up smack against a wall. There were walls around all his thoughts, and he seemed utterly unaware of them, though he was perpetually hiding behind them.” </i>
</blockquote><br /><b>On the ends not justifying the means:</b><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“One need only act, without fear of punishment and without hope of reward: act from the center of one's soul.” </i>
</blockquote><br /><b>On the necessity of hope: </b><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“I thought I knew what ‘realism’ was,” Keng said. She smiled, but it was not an easy smile.<br /><br />“How can you, if you don’t know what hope is?” </i>
</blockquote><br /><b>On uniqueness: </b><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“They say there is nothing new under any sun. But if each life is not new, each single life, then why are we born?” </i>
</blockquote><br /><b>And, of course, on political revolution: </b><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“You cannot buy the Revolution. You cannot make the Revolution. You can only be the Revolution. It is in your spirit, or it is nowhere.” </i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Needless to say, <i>The Dispossessed</i> won me over completely. In addition to a well-paced, engaging plot and genuinely sympathetic characters, it also contained some truly fascinating political and philosophical discussions, and examined the human condition compassionately <i>and</i> candidly without getting overly sentimental or saccharine. Plus, it was actually <b>fun</b> to read! Le Guin handled it all so deftly and subtly that even the political ideology, such as it was, never really grated on my nerves or felt preachy. So if you’re ever in the mood for science fiction that you can connect with on both an emotional and an intellectual level, try this book on for size. Ursula K. Le Guin really knew what’s up.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13575707432309935772" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2787957147" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2787957147" />
    <a title="J.L.   Sutton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3675162-j-l-sutton"><img alt="J.L.   Sutton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438142892p2/3675162.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2787957147?book_show_action=true">May 31, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.L.   Sutton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.L.   Sutton" href="/user/show/3675162-j-l-sutton">J.L.   Sutton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2787957147" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17062574766331092054">Ursula Le Guin's The Dispossessed was a difficult book for me to review. On the one hand, I like the ideas Le Guin explores and the fact that she doesn't take shortcuts. Her depictions of utopia, for instance, are interesting and thought provoking, but at the same time they're obviously flawed. That's not a surprise. Still, it's frustrating for the reader that the main character, Shevek, doesn't see those flaws. Even though it's probably perfectly natural for people in a given cultural context n</span>
  <span id="freeText17062574766331092054" style="display:none">Ursula Le Guin's The Dispossessed was a difficult book for me to review. On the one hand, I like the ideas Le Guin explores and the fact that she doesn't take shortcuts. Her depictions of utopia, for instance, are interesting and thought provoking, but at the same time they're obviously flawed. That's not a surprise. Still, it's frustrating for the reader that the main character, Shevek, doesn't see those flaws. Even though it's probably perfectly natural for people in a given cultural context not to recognize societal mechanisms that are pressuring them to act in this or that way, it makes it difficult to connect to him as a character. That, and the juxtaposed earlier story, makes getting into the flow of The Dispossessed difficult. Our book group figured that this didn't happen until maybe page 131. <br /><br />Not sure precisely when it happened, but I ended up liking this and began rereading it. It was great that the story came full circle, but I'm not sure the reader experiences this without at least beginning a reread. I'm told that the Hainish Cycle is very different, but I'm intrigued about how the worlds connect. So I enjoyed this but there are some qualifications here. 3.25 stars. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17062574766331092054" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_261275499" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/261275499" />
    <a title="Megan Baxter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5742086-megan-baxter"><img alt="Megan Baxter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1310153693p2/5742086.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/261275499?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Megan Baxter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Megan Baxter" href="/user/show/5742086-megan-baxter">Megan Baxter</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer261275499" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer1272493061912896224">Human nature tends towards, not entropy, but bureaucracy.<br/><br/>I fear my review might focus more on Anarres and less on Urras, as it was the Anarrian sections that interested me more, the attempts to sustain (founding was the easy part) an anarcho-syndicalist society over a long period of time. For Urras, I thought that Urras was painted in clear terms, and avoided a polemic, although it did have very pointed things to say about class, and war, and conscription, and property, and the gendering thereof</span>
  <span id="freeText1272493061912896224" style="display:none">Human nature tends towards, not entropy, but bureaucracy.<br /><br />I fear my review might focus more on Anarres and less on Urras, as it was the Anarrian sections that interested me more, the attempts to sustain (founding was the easy part) an anarcho-syndicalist society over a long period of time. <div id="review_1736036117" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1736036117" />
    <a title="Simona B" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5312173-simona-b"><img alt="Simona B" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1408893128p2/5312173.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1736036117?book_show_action=true">Aug 22, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simona B" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simona B" href="/user/show/5312173-simona-b">Simona B</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5312173-simona-b?shelf=in-english">in-english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5312173-simona-b?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5312173-simona-b?shelf=1900">1900</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1736036117" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6762048080069712750"><b>"He has power over you. Where does he get it from? Not from vested authority, there isn't any. Not from intellectual excellence, he hasn't any. He gets it from the innate cowardice of the average human mind. Public opinion! That's the power structure he's part of, and knows how to use."</b><br/><br/>I have decided this won't be a proper review, but rather a short, focused comment.<br/><br/><i>The Dispossessed</i> is one of those books written not to make you lose yourself in the story, but to make you yourself in your thought</span>
  <span id="freeText6762048080069712750" style="display:none"><b>"He has power over you. Where does he get it from? Not from vested authority, there isn't any. Not from intellectual excellence, he hasn't any. He gets it from the innate cowardice of the average human mind. Public opinion! That's the power structure he's part of, and knows how to use."</b><br /><br />I have decided this won't be a proper review, but rather a short, focused comment.<br /><br /><i>The Dispossessed</i> is one of those books written not to make you lose yourself in the story, but to make you yourself in your thoughts. The aim of this book is to make you think, because this book is an <b>experiment</b>.<br />Ursula K. Le Guin wanted to write a dystopia. But she resolved to do so in the absence of any superior form of governement, which, had it been present, should have supposedly been oppressive/abusive. As a lover of the dystopian genre, I find Le Guin's idea particularly <b>stimulating</b>, because it carries to the extreme a condition that we find in almost every dystopian novel and without which a totalitarian regime would be not onlt powerless, but thoroughly unable to survive, and that is <b>habit</b>. When people obey so efficiently, it's mainly because <b>obeying has become customary</b>; so much so that no one notices anymore that they are, after all, obeying. So this is what Le Guin conceived: let's imagina a society where there is no law, no religion, and where the people's submission comes from an intimate, deeply-rooted <i>desire</i> to live like this, to favor the society's requests (<i>requests</i>; never orders) to their own needs. And smarter yet it's that "society" is never truly acknowledged like a real thing, there being no true for of government -this people describes itself as anarchist. How anyone think to rebel against <i>that</i>?<br /><br />The disconcerting thing about <i>The Dispossessed</i> is precisely this. <b>Everyone thinks they are doing only what they please, when they please, how they please</b>. When put like this, in a sense, it's all very similar to Huxley's <i>Brave New World</i> (one of my favorite novels of all times) only in reversed circumstances.<br /><br />My main, big problem with <b>
  <i>The Dispossessed</i>
</b> it's that it <b>often felt like a theoretical book, or like an essay</b>, more than like a work of fiction. Too many and too long descriptive passages -descriptive of the <i>ideas</i>, of course- and too little about what this all means in practicality. <b>I like to see how ideas germinate, not only to be told how</b>. In this sense, I loved Le Guin's writing style, but I do no agree with <i>how</i> she chose to write this book, which is why I did not enjoy it as much as I could have. It's a little disheartening, I admit. But it was really worth a chance.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6762048080069712750" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText9374488016220610596" style="display:none">If you're into stuff like this, <a href="http://manuelaantao.blogspot.com/2016/11/anarres-vs-urras-ambiguous-walls.html" rel="nofollow noopener">you can read the full review.</a><br /><br />Anarres vs. Urras - Ambiguous Walls: “The Dispossessed” by Ursula K. But I got drunk at a party, ejaculated all over a woman's dress (Did Bill Clinton read my tale?) and then promptly threw up. Did you skip some of my story? I am not amused! What are you going to do with him?' and so on...but I won't dwell on the point. ;) And now having read the novel again after about 10 years, I am even fonder of it that I was back then. Chapter 5 is like a distilled version of “The Brothers Karamazov” and the whole is a more serious, thoughtful “Stranger in a Strange Land”.<br /> <br />So, yes, Manuel, Shevek is quite like me in many ways, but since I've never had a single alcoholic drink in my life, I am even more unreliable as a character!!<br />And so this orphan is all alone without even a cardboard character to keep me company. ;)"<br /> <br />If you're into vintage SF, read the rest of the review elsewhere.</span>

<div id="review_207766202" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/207766202" />
    <a title="Aubrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey"><img alt="Aubrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472535045p2/4119460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/207766202?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aubrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aubrey" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey">Aubrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780380003822" href="/book/show/894464.The_Dispossessed">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reality-check">reality-check</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=5-star">5-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=books-are-the-best-invention">books-are-the-best-invention</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-2013">r-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=prose-prose-prose">prose-prose-prose</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1-read-on-hand">1-read-on-hand</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads">r-goodreads</a>,

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer207766202" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9889971278317175386"><blockquote>
  <i>Fulfillment, Shevek thought, is a function of time. The search for pleasure is circular, repetitive, atemporal. The variety seeking of the spectator, the thrill hunter, the sexually promiscuous, always ends in the same place. It has an end. It comes to the end and has to start over. It is not a journey and return, but a closed cycle, a locked room, a cell.<br/><br/>Outside the locked room is the landscape of time, in which the spirit may, with luck and courage, construct the fragile, makeshift, improba</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText9889971278317175386" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>Fulfillment, Shevek thought, is a function of time. The search for pleasure is circular, repetitive, atemporal. The variety seeking of the spectator, the thrill hunter, the sexually promiscuous, always ends in the same place. It has an end. It comes to the end and has to start over. It is not a journey and return, but a closed cycle, a locked room, a cell.<br /><br />Outside the locked room is the landscape of time, in which the spirit may, with luck and courage, construct the fragile, makeshift, improbable roads and cities of fidelity: a landscape inhabitable by human beings.<br /><br />It is not until an act occurs within the landscape of the past and the future that it is a human act. Loyalty, which asserts the continuity of past and future, binding time into a whole, is the root of human strength; there is no good to be done without it.</i>
</blockquote>I am a selfish and impractical person when it comes to many things in life, and literature is no exception. It's not enough for the book to be worthy in its own merit, whatever attributes of prose, plot, parcel of fact and fiction it brings to the table for my judgment. For I will judge, but more than that, I will steal and stow away any piece and passage that is worthy of my doing so. More than that, I will build my own string of words and fancies off the standard structure, disguising them under that formulaic word of 'review' and letting them loose on the grandest forum technology has thus far provided. Simply put, appreciation is no longer enough, if there is no room for me to create.<br /><br />Not a very stable concept, is it? To take a work of seeming completeness and force it into strange and subjective shapes, to be unsatisfied with sitting back and letting the simple phrase of 'This is what I thought of the book...' speak for the full experience of that particular reading. To distrust the shortcuts of words, the 'speculative fiction', the 'Ursula K. Le Guin', all those key terminology characteristics that first attracted me to considering the work worthy of my attention.<br /><br />Worthy of my attention? What is that? What is my 'attention', per se, one that has made a business of malcontent with institutions, ideologies, and has given up any hope of finding refuge in any one-time ready-made form, anything that does not shift and slither and mutate with every passing second, season, generation?<br /><br />Exactly that, really. For I am a creature bound by time and its limited infinity, its cyclical progress, its complex web where the facts are as driven by the abstract as by the scientific method, however much people would like to believe otherwise. A person who, by the matters of coincidence and consequence, will be easily won over by any piece of media that considers science as beautiful as it considers art. A simple matter, one would think, but also a lie of simplification, deceptively mundane if one doesn't mention examples of successful media. <i>The Magic Mountain</i>, by Thomas Mann. The works of Pynchon. Others that I cannot recall for fault of my own limited recollection, a thought as fascinating and as frightful as the void itself, for how much is holding my own opinions worth when I cannot even keep all the parameters of it within the limits of instantaneous retrieval forevermore? And this one, one that's forced me to further expand and complicate my definition of what I 'like', as evidenced by the disparity in complex depth between it and the former works.<br /><br />An excellent thought experiment when it comes to the 'lie of simplification', really. For this book does not attempt to achieve reality through constant bombardment of ideas, or strain the lines of connective tissue between branches of knowledge to the point of nonsensical pride and fearful rejection. Instead, you have the simple elegance of a mathematical proof, bound up in the terms of biological fact and empathetic concern, solving the factor of an ever progressing instant with an ever widening calcification of history behind it, on a ship of constants built by the dead in hopes for the living.<br /><br />The answer? You'd have a better chance of finding stabilized truth in the Uncertainty Principle, where change is not a means to the end, but one and the same. A theorem that, despite its incontrovertible reality, has not yet led the entire scientific community into a mass existential crisis, but has also not yet led the public consciousness to the realization that there is inherent futility in seeking a life within a label. Of course, the labels that are inherently tied up with a consideration for the unceasing flow of time and all the resulting chaos of such a concept are likely to do better. But the fact of the matter remains that, with every new self, every forward momentum of knowledge and insight, from a child on one side of the world gazing at a cave painting daubed in some unknown past millenia, to a child on the other grasping the instantaneous crossfire of the Internet faster than its makers, the labels are no more than a guideline. Easily fitted, easily discarded, easily broken down and cannibalized by those who only have use for bits and pieces of it. A constant evolution frightening for the majority of those who wish only for balance with the existing structure, and will not or cannot afford to sacrifice livelihood for the inevitability of progress.<br /><br />A difficult situation, for those who have equally invested their interests in the maintenance of their self and the future of humanity. A simultaneous transience, that requires an ever watchful eye on the facts of the world at large as well as the delicate clockwork of the only mind at one's disposal. An effort sustained by the thought that the effort will never be complete, with the kinder words of uniqueness and altruism inextricably bound up with those of egotism and naivety, where one kith and kind of knowledgeable insight will never, truly, be enough, no matter how much compartmentalization the simple act of socializing in theorized, standardized, economized forms demands. <br /><br />I read to discover, and write in full knowledge that the discovery will never be enough. I am grateful for what the world can give me, and criticize in full knowledge of my ungratefulness. I balance my consumption with my delivery, and find as much glory in the cold curves of physics as I do in the complex vagaries of philosophy. I am here, and if you try to convince me of a label, or attempt to label me, I will look, and listen, and give you as much benefit of a doubt as fits within the society defined realms of politeness and my personal realms of social behavior. Keep in mind, though, the constant give and take of truth and empathy will always be more fruitful than the demand for an unchanging acquiescence to anything at all, much as the simplistic equations of calculable chemistry disguise a fierce and frothing equilibrium at every level of growth and decay. If you insist otherwise, I will drift away, for I am a selfish and impractical person when it comes my maxim that conventions are never enough, and all my efforts to learn will always hold equal footing with my valuing of personal sensibilities of what it means to be human.<blockquote>
  <i>"My race is very old," Ketho said. "We have been civilized for a thousand millenia. We have histories of hundreds of those millenia. We have tried everything. Anarchism, with the rest. But <b>I</b> have not tried it. They say there is nothing new under any sun. But if each life is not new, each single life, then why are we born?"</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="9889971278317175386" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_579239245" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/579239245" />
    <a title="Stuart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart"><img alt="Stuart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452041473p2/9456990.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/579239245?book_show_action=true">Apr 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stuart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stuart" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart">Stuart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=politics">politics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=humanistic-sf">humanistic-sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer579239245" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12386230559523731136"><b>The Dispossessed: Not simply an anarchist utopia/capitalist dystopia</b><br/>Originally posted at <a href='http://www.fantasyliterature.com/reviews/the-dispossessed/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fantasy Literature</a><br/>The Dispossessed is a perfectly achieved thought experiment, perhaps Ursula K. Le Guin’s greatest work, but there is little I can say that hasn’t been said more eloquently, forcefully, thoroughly, or knowledgeably by other reviewers. It transcends the genre as a Novel of Ideas. It explores with great intelligence anarchism-socialism vs capitalism; freedom/slavery in terms of politics, econo</span>
  <span id="freeText12386230559523731136" style="display:none"><b>The Dispossessed: Not simply an anarchist utopia/capitalist dystopia</b><br />Originally posted at <a href="http://www.fantasyliterature.com/reviews/the-dispossessed/" rel="nofollow noopener">Fantasy Literature</a><br />The Dispossessed is a perfectly achieved thought experiment, perhaps Ursula K. Le Guin’s greatest work, but there is little I can say that hasn’t been said more eloquently, forcefully, thoroughly, or knowledgeably by other reviewers. It transcends the genre as a Novel of Ideas. It explores with great intelligence anarchism-socialism vs capitalism; freedom/slavery in terms of politics, economics, society, intellectual endeavor, and personal relationships; the struggle to perfect a scientific theory that unifies time and space; whether human nature inevitably corrupts all political ideals; whether political utopias can ever be achieved to a meaningful degree; whether only hardship and privation can support socialist sentiment; and whether we must therefore settle for capitalism and the pursuit of possessions, money, and power.<br /><br />Having just read all the many insightful reviews of this towering novel, which remains humble, grounded, and refuses to provide easy answers where there are none, I don’t think I have much to add to the body of discussion and analysis The Dispossessed has generated in the almost half-century since its publication. Rather, I think it has achieved its goal of perfectly integrating a story of Big Ideas with the profoundly-moving story of physicist Shevek in his struggle to pursue his theories in the arid anarchist society of Annares and later in the capitalist societies of the more abundant Urras. It is also a surprisingly powerful love story about Shevek and his partner Takver, as they try to navigate the difficulty of living their ideals within a less-than-perfect anarchist society in which the needs of the group take precedence over those of the individual.<br /><br />What is really impressive about this book is that is remains gripping and thought-provoking despite a lack of almost any kinetic action or intricacies of plot. It truly rewards the patient reader who wishes to contemplate the ideas presented and examine them in terms of their own life experiences, compare them with their understanding of the political systems that govern their own society, and what aspects could realistically be changed for the better. After all, considering what happened in the disastrous US election in 2016, I don’t think anyone out there could for a moment claim that the US political system is the pinnacle of human achievement.<br /><br />Having said that, without any viable anarchist social experiments to point to beyond kibbutzes in the desert, it’s hard to say whether that model could ever exist on a large scale in the real world. And that is exactly what The Dispossessed sets out to do — depict a realistic, non-idealized anarchist society that functions with the full participation of its members maintaining continuous revolution on a daily basis. And by showing the flaws and inevitable creep toward bureaucracy, conformism, and petty power struggles, Le Guin is willing to put her anarchist society under stress tests that raise legitimate questions of its viability.<br /><br />In the end, it’s quite easy for us to see the flaws of capitalism given that it relies on greed, self-interest, consumption, private possessions, and competition between social classes, but it is another thing entirely to create a fictional anarchist utopia and put it through the ringer within the context of a compelling story of real people with complex motivations and ideals. It would be easy to dismiss such a thought experiment as hopelessly idealistic or irrelevant, but I think that would be to miss the point of this book. Le Guin’s book is not a call to arms to discard our corrupt capitalist systems and form anarchist or socialist communities with The Dispossessed as our Little Red Book. Instead, she wants us to examine our own lives and political structures and think about what ideals really should drive society, and then what aspects of our political and social systems should or can be improved upon, since we can all agree they are far from perfect.<br /><br />Sadly, even when such a hypothetical society is composed of people dedicated to such political ideals as Annares is, it still struggles to hold together over time. It is this willingness to accept the fallibility of all political systems that is Le Guin’s most mature sentiment, one sorely lacking among demagogues or jingo-chanting nationalists. It is the refusal to simplify things that makes The Dispossessed both intellectually-stimulating and politically-unsatisfying, since it is unlikely to galvanize us to action, but rather pushes us to reflect on our own assumptions about ourselves and our societies. It is a masterpiece in the genre and a great book by any measure, one of my all-time favorites.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12386230559523731136" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1817045682" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1817045682" />
    <a title="Peter Boyle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1903072-peter-boyle"><img alt="Peter Boyle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1422298189p2/1903072.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1817045682?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter Boyle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter Boyle" href="/user/show/1903072-peter-boyle">Peter Boyle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1817045682" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15526979340966944894">My hero <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6538289.David_Mitchell' title='David Mitchell' rel='nofollow noopener'>David Mitchell</a> frequently mentions The Dispossessed as one of his favourite books, and it is regularly described as one of the most significant sci-fi novels of all time. So I just HAD to see what all the fuss was about.<br/><br/>The story revolves around two distant planets, Urras and Annares. Years ago a rebellion brewed on Urras and in order to avoid conflict, the anarchists left to start a new life on Annares. Urras is a bountiful place with a capitalist, patriarchal society, whereas Annares i</span>
  <span id="freeText15526979340966944894" style="display:none">My hero <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6538289.David_Mitchell" title="David Mitchell" rel="nofollow noopener">David Mitchell</a> frequently mentions The Dispossessed as one of his favourite books, and it is regularly described as one of the most significant sci-fi novels of all time. So I just HAD to see what all the fuss was about.<br /><br />The story revolves around two distant planets, Urras and Annares. Years ago a rebellion brewed on Urras and in order to avoid conflict, the anarchists left to start a new life on Annares. Urras is a bountiful place with a capitalist, patriarchal society, whereas Annares is a mostly barren planet with a collectivist community. The two worlds have a bit of strained relationship: Annares considers itself independent despite its vital trade links with Urras, while Urras thinks of its sister planet as a backward mining colony. At the centre of events is Shevek, a brilliant Anarresti physicist. He travels to Urras to share and develop revolutionary scientific theories, and through his eyes we see the benefits and drawbacks of life on each world.<br /><br />This is a novel overflowing with ideas but not a lot of plot. It sets up the concept of 'an ambiguous utopia' - the inhabitants of each world see their own societies as ideal. Urras, though rich and prosperous, suffers from inequality and war, while Annares is a peaceful community with uncomfortable living conditions. Le Guin examines both civilisations cogently and we come to realise the flaws of both systems. But I was hoping for more action, more excitement. Shevek is the only character who is fully developed and I didn't even find him that interesting. Nothing really happens until the last quarter of the story and by then it was too late to stir my enthusiasm. I can see why The Dispossessed is regarded as an important book and it is certainly thought-provoking. But I just didn't love it as I much as I had hoped.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15526979340966944894" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1362963605" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1362963605" />
    <a title="Gabrielle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6753400-gabrielle"><img alt="Gabrielle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598976963p2/6753400.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1362963605?book_show_action=true">Aug 13, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gabrielle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gabrielle" href="/user/show/6753400-gabrielle">Gabrielle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781857988826" href="/book/show/756970.The_Dispossessed">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=mandatory-reading">mandatory-reading</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=desert-island">desert-island</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=own-a-copy">own-a-copy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=own-multiple-editions">own-multiple-editions</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=read-in-2015">read-in-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=speculative-fiction">speculative-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=read-in-2019">read-in-2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1362963605" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer824829237419734822">Updated review after a re-read in November 2019.<br/><br/>---<br/><br/>“Change is freedom, change is life."<br/><br/>"It's always easier not to think for oneself. Find a nice safe hierarchy and settle in. Don't make changes, don't risk disapproval, don't upset your syndics. It's always easiest to let yourself be governed."<br/><br/>"There's a point, around age twenty, when you have to choose whether to be like everybody else the rest of your life, or to make a virtue of your peculiarities."<br/><br/>"Those who build walls are their own prisoner</span>
  <span id="freeText824829237419734822" style="display:none">Updated review after a re-read in November 2019.<br /><br />---<br /><br />“Change is freedom, change is life."<br /><br />"It's always easier not to think for oneself. Find a nice safe hierarchy and settle in. Don't make changes, don't risk disapproval, don't upset your syndics. It's always easiest to let yourself be governed."<br /><br />"There's a point, around age twenty, when you have to choose whether to be like everybody else the rest of your life, or to make a virtue of your peculiarities."<br /><br />"Those who build walls are their own prisoners. I'm going to go fulfill my proper function in the social organism. I'm going to go unbuild walls.” <br /><br />This novel will for ever be one of my favorite books: when graceful, intelligent prose and brave, nuanced ideas collide into one great story that intertwines the personal and the political, you get a treasure like “The Dispossessed”. This book jumped at the top of my favorites list mere seconds after I finished the last line.<br /><br />Shevek was born and raised on the anarchist colony of Anarres, and while he has always embraced the principles on which his society was founded, as his work in physics becomes more complicated, challenging and promising, he begins to see cracks in the utopic system his ancestors created. A visit to the twin (but capitalist) planet Urras brings into sharp relief the differences between the two worlds, but also brings to light more commonalities than Shevek had expected. He soon finds himself caught in a high stake political game that would seek to make him the figurehead of a new revolution – or let him take the fall for its failure, depending on who has the upper hand.<br /><br />LeGuin built her story carefully, and the two narratives, one set on Urras and one on Annarres, feed each other and collide at the perfect moment to bring the story together flawlessly. Brilliant narrative structure aside, this book is simply stuffed with beautiful and thought-provoking passages I had to stop and re-read a few times.<br /><br />It would be a gross over-simplification to say that this is a sci-fi book about communism. Yes, it is that, but it is so much more. It is a nuanced, idealistic, heartbreaking, gentle and extremely intelligent novel. The subtitle “Ambiguous Utopia” is perfect: a book like that challenges the reader without ever trying to preach to them, letting them make their own minds up about the fictional anarcho-communist planet of Annares and its relation to its capitalist home world of Urras.<br /><br />Shevek is one of the most beautifully rendered characters I’ve encountered. Stuck between both worlds, he struggles with the philosophies he lived his whole life by, the advantages of the new world he is discovering and his longing for what he left behind. He is flawed and lost, but also incredibly wise and brave, with a strong sense of compassion and integrity. I just loved him. And unexpectedly, I found his relationship with his partner Takver to be deeply romantic.<br /><br />Le Guin definitely preached to the choir in terms of politics with me, I admit it. But I admired the fearlessness with which she chose to point out that whatever system of wealth distribution you live in, people will try to exploit each other, people will bully and ostracize those who don’t fit quite right with their herd, people will feel jealously and hatred. People on Annares share the wealth and the work, but they are still humans, with all the good and negative connotations that entails. This is why her utopia is ambiguous: human nature remains no matter what system you place it in and while you can dream of giving people a better life by giving them a system or code to get rid of inequalities, you can never remove the wild card of “people and how they will behave” from the equation.<br /><br />I believe this book to be a classic, and I believe it completely transcends the science-fiction label. It is nothing less than a great work of art in my eyes and I recommend it to everyone.<br /><br />"You cannot buy the Revolution. You cannot make the Revolution. You can only be the Revolution. It is in your spirit or it is nowhere."</span>
  <a data-text-id="824829237419734822" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText9690441013682677017" style="display:none">Contentment generally doesn’t compel writers to their greatest work. Historian <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/137261.Barbara_W__Tuchman" title="Barbara W. Tuchman" rel="nofollow noopener">Barbara Tuchman</a> mentions this in one of her books. I think it’s true for fiction, too. The happiness of others— making babies, seeing friends, tying one on, etc— is a bore. Even if it’s happening a million years in the future on two adjacent planets 20 light years away. The writing however is far better than the content, which is why I give it 3 stars. There can be no question that here at least Le Guin has produced excellent genre based literary fiction. It’s a novel of ideas, very much based on mid-twentieth century problems: anarchism, police states, capitalism, espionage, revolutionaries, the common weal. I found it a rather dreary recapitalization of some very old and tired ideas. Though there can be no doubting the zeal with which it was written, I skipped the last fifty pages.</span>

<div id="review_2210127432" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2210127432" />
    <a title="Fran" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/74816651-fran"><img alt="Fran" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586773357p2/74816651.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2210127432?book_show_action=true">Dec 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fran" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fran" href="/user/show/74816651-fran">Fran</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/74816651-fran?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/74816651-fran?shelf=speculative-fiction">speculative-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/74816651-fran?shelf=adult">adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/74816651-fran?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/74816651-fran?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2210127432" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9315042499605619927">One of the things that always comes to my mind when reading this novel (yes, I’ve read it several times) is this feeling that while Anarres does represent something that feels like a utopian future, it’s a utopia/dystopia of a different kind, one that feels the closets to a future that may work for everyone.<br/><br/>Anarres is a small moon, colonized by humans from the planet Urras. For centuries, Urra’s government contained and served only the wealthy. And the wealthy loathe the poor. So, by ensuring th</span>
  <span id="freeText9315042499605619927" style="display:none">One of the things that always comes to my mind when reading this novel (yes, I’ve read it several times) is this feeling that while Anarres does represent something that feels like a utopian future, it’s a utopia/dystopia of a different kind, one that feels the closets to a future that may work for everyone.<br /><br />Anarres is a small moon, colonized by humans from the planet Urras. For centuries, Urra’s government contained and served only the wealthy. And the wealthy loathe the poor. So, by ensuring the masses received only the minimum of education and state support, the wealthy have ensured that no one born in poverty can’t escape it. If this sounds just like the world today, it’s not a coincidence. With academic anthropologists Alfred and Theodora Kroeber for parents and with J. Robert Oppenheimer as close family friend, Le Guin had a clear understanding of the way society works and what the near future would become as fewer came to posses most of the wealth. Moreover, as the excellent science fiction writer she was, she not only predicted the car but the traffic jam as well. <br /><br />Anyhow, in this world of Urras, public health services had been obliterated, so those who cannot afford private care are crammed into filthy hospitals where they go to die, and any protests are put down with brutal force. That is, until the inescapable inequality provokes an anger that can be repressed. So, little more than a century before the story opens, there’s a huge revolution. Many are killed, but the people keep marching and fighting, so instead of keep killing them, or hazarded being killed by them, the oppressors offer the revolutionary an option: to set up their own ideal society in one of Urras’s orbiting moons. <br /><br />That moon is Anarres, where the idealists set up an anarchistic society based on principles of shared wealth, shared responsibility and shared lives. Superficially, the society works. But by the time we are slung into the story, things have stagnated. The revolution is in the past. There’s no government, but other institutions play similar roles, and within them new ideas are frowned upon, even feared, and self-interested people have started to hog power. In this new society knowledge is the highest currency, and new, significant, evolving knowledge is all the more dangerous for those who stay in power by presentencing themselves as the all-knowing (who, not surprising, seem to be theoretical physicists). So once again, discontent is brewing.<br /><br />Among the discontent is Shevek, a brilliant young physicist, who finds his work blocked by a jealous superior, as his theories conflict with the prevailing political philosophy and are therefore disruptive. Nonetheless, is because of such theories that Shevek is granted permission to travel from Anarres to Urras. As expected, Shevek has grown disillusioned with the anarchists of Anarres, and such emotions serve to highlight his even more intense disillusionment of the government (and scientists) of Urras, who want to monopolize his theory for its economic value. <br /><br />Knowing Le Guin’s belief in writing serving more than one purpose, the way in which she structured her book—chapters alternate between the two planets and between past and future—must be significant. And it is, because as Shevek's big physics breakthrough the "theory of simultaneity” plays with ideas of time, beginnings and endings, and the past happening at the same time as the present, the book’s the structure becomes meaningful as a constant reminder of such theory, and by extension, of the importance of it being recognized.<br /><br />Similarly, Shevek's journey has a pleasing circularity, from Anarres to Urras and back. And there’s intellectual substance in Le Guin's portrayal of how, given the right conditions, an anarchist society might function and malfunction. And it all happens while we metaphorically run toward a fast paced climax that is hard to imagine but extremely satisfying.<br /><br />Nonetheless, what brings me back to this book time after time, is this sense of reality enshrouded in possibility. All these very real characters, living very real lives, suffering, loving, laughing, missing and encountering each other within a world that is not real but that feels as real as the world around us. No doubt this remains a challenging and urgent book, and a good reminder of the interplay between human nature and power. And let’s not forget that this is all exquisitely written, in a prose that flows and is engaging, and where each word has earned its place.<br /><br />Now, anybody who knows me knows I’m a big fan of Ursula K. Le Guin. That’s no secret. But don’t let my fangirl-ism fool you, because whatever good thing I said about this book is well-deserved and you should read it. In fact you should read all of Le Guin's works, her books, and her essays, and her poetry, and her short stories. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9315042499605619927" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2826002899" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8496621489809199324"><b> Do I like this book?</b><br/><br/>I'm really not sure. Maybe I should have followed the GR reading order, as at the start I was very confused. But this tale of two separate planets, seen through the eyes of the intelligent & (originally) idealistic Shevek parallels our own society. Communism or Capitalism? Which system is better? <br/><br/> I particularly like this quote:<br/><br/><blockquote>
  <i>“My world, my Earth is a ruin. A planet spoiled by the human species. We multiplied and fought and gobbled until there was nothing left, and then </i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText8496621489809199324" style="display:none"><b> Do I like this book?</b><br><br>I'm really not sure. Maybe I should have followed the GR reading order, as at the start I was very confused. But this tale of two separate planets, seen through the eyes of the intelligent &amp; (originally) idealistic Shevek parallels our own society. Communism or Capitalism? Which system is better? <br><br> I particularly like this quote:<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>“My world, my Earth is a ruin. A planet spoiled by the human species. We multiplied and fought and gobbled until there was nothing left, and then we died. We controlled neither appetite nor violence; we did not adapt. We destroyed ourselves. But we destroyed the world first.</i>
</blockquote><br><br>The whole impact of the book took me over then. Overall, this book was a far better read for me from 70% on.<br><br><i>Read with The Women's Classic Literature Group.</i><br><br><blockquote>
  <a href="https://imgur.com/59uqgVc" rel="nofollow noopener">
    <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1547280079i/26905161.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
  </a>
